<div id="review_2550777667" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2550777667" />
    <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud"><img alt="Oleksandr Zholud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506182069p2/4743119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2550777667?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oleksandr Zholud" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud">Oleksandr Zholud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35422580-babel-17">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2550777667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11093377508602456218">This is the second book by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/49111.Samuel_R__Delany' title='Samuel R. Delany' rel='nofollow noopener'>Samuel R. Delany</a>, which I’ve read, the first being <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/145354.The_Einstein_Intersection' title='The Einstein Intersection by Samuel R. Delany' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Einstein Intersection</a>. Both books won Nebula Awards, both are with clearly 60s feel in them, a bit trippy and clearly poetic, but I liked this one more.<br/><br/>The story follows Rydra Wong, an extremely capable linguist in her search regarding a new and strange language, labeled Babel-17, which is presumably linked to sabotage and attacks against the Alliance – her side in eternal intergalactic battle. The book never menti</span>
  <span id="freeText11093377508602456218" style="display:none">This is the second book by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/49111.Samuel_R__Delany" title="Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">Samuel R. Delany</a>, which I’ve read, the first being <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/145354.The_Einstein_Intersection" title="The Einstein Intersection by Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">The Einstein Intersection</a>. Both books won Nebula Awards, both are with clearly 60s feel in them, a bit trippy and clearly poetic, but I liked this one more.<br /><br />The story follows Rydra Wong, an extremely capable linguist in her search regarding a new and strange language, labeled Babel-17, which is presumably linked to sabotage and attacks against the Alliance – her side in eternal intergalactic battle. The book never mentions it by name, but it is clearly based on the assumption of <i>linguistic determinism</i> also known as a strong form of linguistic relativity or the Sapir–Whorf hypothesis, which argues that individuals experience the world based on the structure of the language they habitually use. <br /><br />While the novel clearly has the strong message basis, that what we can think depends on the language we use, it is much more than that. It has its unique version of FTL travel with captains, so changed by plastic surgery that they more resemble fantastic beasts than humans; three navigators, who form a triple – analogue to a married couple and supply information about hyperspace by different senses (so they are called eye, ear and nose); discorporates – ghost from machines, uploaded consciousnesses; a bunch of kids to repair a ship (a very XIX century concept of kids small enough to run errands within giant machines).<br /><br />The prose is both smooth and strangely gripping. I’m not a fan of poetry, have something of a poetry-deafness, but I can state that this is clearly a poetic prose, a similar feel to what I get from reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/874602.Ursula_K__Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>, even if their styles are quite different. An important milestone of SF genre, but not an easy read, it calls for thinking the material over and over, hardly an escapist yarn.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11093377508602456218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Tamahome" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2768410-tamahome"><img alt="Tamahome" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338998570p2/2768410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/825857942?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamahome" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamahome" href="/user/show/2768410-tamahome">Tamahome</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20458507-babel-17">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer825857942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14431330522281337435">Jo Walton's take: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.tor.com/blogs/2009/06/still-exciting-today-samuel-delanys-babel-17'>http://www.tor.com/blogs/2009/06/stil...</a><br/><br/><br/>12.0% "Liked the kindle sample and picked it up. Seems much more likeable than Einstein Intersection. :)"<br/>17.0% ""He was unkempt and towheaded and sapphire-eyed, but the only cosmetisurgery evident was the bright rose growing on his shoulder.""<br/>35.0% ""The discorporate crew deperceptualized.""<br/>50.0% "I'm swimming in language theory, and it's cool."<br/>81.0% "Now it's being weird."<br/><br/><br/>All done. I think I liked the first half better than the second ha</span>
  <span id="freeText14431330522281337435" style="display:none">Jo Walton's take: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.tor.com/blogs/2009/06/still-exciting-today-samuel-delanys-babel-17">http://www.tor.com/blogs/2009/06/stil...</a><br /><br /><br />12.0% "Liked the kindle sample and picked it up. Seems much more likeable than Einstein Intersection. :)"<br />17.0% ""He was unkempt and towheaded and sapphire-eyed, but the only cosmetisurgery evident was the bright rose growing on his shoulder.""<br />35.0% ""The discorporate crew deperceptualized.""<br />50.0% "I'm swimming in language theory, and it's cool."<br />81.0% "Now it's being weird."<br /><br /><br />All done. I think I liked the first half better than the second half. I believe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92726847" rel="nofollow noopener">I reacted to Nova</a> the same way. Delany is a clever boy, but he's not really interested in creating a mood. I don't know; chasing after the pleasures of Delany is a little bit frustrating. If you like poetry, there's lots of it. I can't complain about the short length. A lot of his stuff is out on ebook now. I don't know how well the experimental text layout in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85867.Dhalgren" title="Dhalgren by Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">Dhalgren</a> is done. Maybe I should check out the end of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7999236.Through_the_Valley_of_the_Nest_of_Spiders" title="Through the Valley of the Nest of Spiders by Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">Through the Valley of the Nest of Spiders</a> and skip the gay sexy parts.<br /><br />At least it's real space opera. Not exactly hard sf, but 'smart soft sf', kind of in the vein of a Harlan Ellison. Parts of it reminded me of Cordwainer Smith, or Ted Chiang's 'Story of Your Life' (language).<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14431330522281337435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3272674629" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3272674629" />
    <a title="Denis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2298109-denis"><img alt="Denis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505535394p2/2298109.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3272674629?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denis" href="/user/show/2298109-denis">Denis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441045945" href="/book/show/37971998-babel-17">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3272674629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10595194595175271348">A short very imaginative 'New Wave' novel published in 1966, by Samuel Delaney, who was merely 23/24 years old at the time. Like PKD, the crew are an extremely odd motley of misfits, including revived dead people who operate in a sort of ghost-like form (I love scifi for this reason, you can get away with almost anything as long as you can sorta justify it).<br/><br/>Like van Vogt's "World of Null-A" (1948), this book is based on a linguistic or semantic theme (in this case, the disproved, "Sapir–Whorf hy</span>
  <span id="freeText10595194595175271348" style="display:none">A short very imaginative 'New Wave' novel published in 1966, by Samuel Delaney, who was merely 23/24 years old at the time. Like PKD, the crew are an extremely odd motley of misfits, including revived dead people who operate in a sort of ghost-like form (I love scifi for this reason, you can get away with almost anything as long as you can sorta justify it).<br /><br />Like van Vogt's "World of Null-A" (1948), this book is based on a linguistic or semantic theme (in this case, the disproved, "Sapir–Whorf hypothesis" - a principle claiming that the structure of a language affects its speakers' world view or cognition, and thus people's perceptions are relative to their spoken language - rather than Null-A's General Semantics theme). Like Null-A, it also makes the occasional use of epigraphs. In this case Delany takes his from the work of his then poet wife Marilyn Hacker, who may well have been an inspiration for this novel with and featuring a female poet, telepath and ship's captain as a protagonist (most might consider their spouse as having some form of telepathic abilities) . The goal of the mission here is decoding the language being broadcast by a strange and malicious alien race that is influencing throughout the galaxy.<br /><br />Overall, it is a wonderful example of mid 60's scifi, though stylistically, due to Delany's typical floral prose, I lost a bit of the plot along the way. Creative, enjoyable and one I may well revisit. <br /><br />Note: A few years after finishing this novel (circa 2012), I saw the film "Arrival" and thought, 'Hey, wait just one minute here... this reminds me of...'and realized I had forgotten to review this book, and thus why I am finally getting to it now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10595194595175271348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3vnMmd+W6fYun/RaEl1m8slRJY0FQ+GoSn8XHNOEPjr74KvCFua4nc5cqoQWGS+Ki+nQWq68IkAppo9FMdXMaQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MJi1pKAPg6Br3/yn40ml6VGQFYp48pjNAopzZZCZmIUVgdL/aX/Sy4sconnnDeyREyjgXdMNWyVhU+s8cshq1g==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_29780906'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29780906-slavery-s-capitalism?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Slavery&#39;s Capitalism: A New History of American Economic Development<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/97917.Sven_Beckert?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Sven Beckert<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.12 avg rating &mdash; 91 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2016\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer9298237936751139606\">During the nineteenth century, the United States entered the ranks of the world\'s most advanced and dynamic economies. At the same time, the nation sustained an expansive and brutal system of human bondage. This was no mere coincidence. Slavery\'s Cap<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9298237936751139606\" style=\"display:none\">During the nineteenth century, the United States entered the ranks of the world\'s most advanced and dynamic economies. At the same time, the nation sustained an expansive and brutal system of human bondage. This was no mere coincidence. Slavery\'s Capitalism argues for slavery\'s centrality to the emergence of American capitalism in the decades between the Revolution and the Civil War. According to editors Sven Beckert and Seth Rockman, the issue is not whether slavery itself was or was not capitalist but, rather, the impossibility of understanding the nation\'s spectacular pattern of economic development without situating slavery front and center. American capitalism--renowned for its celebration of market competition, private property, and the self-made man--has its origins in an American slavery predicated on the abhorrent notion that human beings could be legally owned and compelled to work under force of violence.\n\nDrawing on the expertise of sixteen scholars who are at the forefront of rewriting the history of American economic development, Slavery\'s Capitalism identifies slavery as the primary force driving key innovations in entrepreneurship, finance, accounting, management, and political economy that are too often attributed to the so-called free market. Approaching the study of slavery as the originating catalyst for the Industrial Revolution and modern capitalism casts new light on American credit markets, practices of offshore investment, and understandings of human capital. Rather than seeing slavery as outside the institutional structures of capitalism, the essayists recover slavery\'s importance to the American economic past and prompt enduring questions about the relationship of market freedom to human freedom.\n\nContributors Edward E. Baptist, Sven Beckert, Daina Ramey Berry, Kathryn Boodry, Alfred L. Brophy, Stephen Chambers, Eric Kimball, John Majewski, Bonnie Martin, Seth Rockman, Daniel B. Rood, Caitlin Rosenthal, Joshua D. John Harrison’s writing has defied categorisation, building worlds both unreal and all-too real, overlapping and interlocking with each other. His stories are replete with fissures and portals into parallel dimensions, unidentified countries and lost lands. But more important than the places they point to are the obsessions that drive the people who so believe in them, characters who spend their lives hunting for, and haunted by, clues and maps that speak to the possibility of somewhere else.\n\nThis selection of stories, drawn from over 50 years of writing, bears witness to that desire for difference: whether following backstreet occultists, amateur philosophers, down-and-outs or refugees, we see our relationship with ‘the other’ in microscopic detail, and share in Harrison’s rejection of the idea that the world, or our understanding of       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/49111.Samuel_R_Delany">About Samuel R. Delany</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/49111.Samuel_R_Delany"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1516722468p3/49111.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/49111.Samuel_R_Delany">Samuel R. Delany
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,415
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1734476469201380633">Samuel Ray Delany, also known as "Chip," is an award-winning American science fiction author. He was born to a prominent black family on April 1, 1942, and raised in Harlem. His mother, Margaret Carey Boyd Delany, was a library clerk in the New York Public Library system. His father, Samuel Ray Delany, Senior, ran a successful Harlem undertaking establishment, Levy & Delany Funeral Home, on 7th Av</span>
  <span id="freeText1734476469201380633" style="display:none">Samuel Ray Delany, also known as "Chip," is an award-winning American science fiction author. He was born to a prominent black family on April 1, 1942, and raised in Harlem. His mother, Margaret Carey Boyd Delany, was a library clerk in the New York Public Library system. His father, Samuel Ray Delany, Senior, ran a successful Harlem undertaking establishment, Levy &amp; Delany Funeral Home, on 7th Avenue, between 1938 and his death in 1960. The family lived in the top two floors of the three-story private house between five- and six-story Harlem apartment buildings. Delany's aunts were Sadie and Bessie Delany; Delany used some of their adventures as the basis for the adventures of his characters Elsie and Corry in the opening novella <i>Atlantis: Model 1924</i> in his book of largely autobiographical stories <i>Atlantis: Three Tales</i>.<br /><br />Delany attended the Dalton School and the Bronx High School of Science, during which he was selected to attend Camp Rising Sun, the Louis August Jonas Foundation's international summer scholarship program. Delany and poet Marilyn Hacker met in high school, and were married in 1961. Their marriage lasted nineteen years. They had a daughter, Iva Hacker-Delany (b. 1974), who spent a decade working in theater in New York City.<br /><br />Delany was a published science fiction author by the age of 20. He published nine well-regarded science fiction novels between 1962 and 1968, as well as several prize-winning short stories (collected in <i>Driftglass</i> [1971] and more recently in <i>Aye, and Gomorrah, and other stories</i> [2002]). His eleventh and most popular novel, <i>Dhalgren</i>, was published in 1975. His main literary project through the late 1970s and 1980s was the <i>Return to Nevèrÿon</i> series, the overall title of the four volumes and also the title of the fourth and final book.<br /><br />Delany has published several autobiographical/semi-autobiographical accounts of his life as a black, gay, and highly dyslexic writer, including his Hugo award winning autobiography, <i>The Motion of Light in Water</i>.<br /><br />Since 1988, Delany has been a professor at several universities. This includes eleven years as a professor of comparative literature at the University of Massachusetts at Amherst, a year and a half as an English professor at the University at Buffalo. He then moved to the English Department of Temple University in 2001, where he has been teaching since. He has had several visiting guest professorships before and during these same years. He has also published several books of criticism, interviews, and essays. In one of his non-fiction books, <i>Times Square Red, Times Square Blue</i> (1999), he draws on personal experience to examine the relationship between the effort to redevelop Times Square and the public sex lives of working-class men, gay and straight, in New York City.<br /><br />In 2007, Delany was the subject of a documentary film, <i>The Polymath, or, The Life and Opinions of Samuel R. Delany, Gentleman</i>. The film debuted on April 25 at the 2007 Tribeca Film Festival.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1734476469201380633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/13612561-babel-17">Quotes from Babel-17</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;ABSTRACT THOUGHTS in a blue room; Nominative, genitive, etative, accusative one, accusative two, ablative, partitive, illative, instructive, abessive, adessive, inessive, essive, allative, translative, comitative. Sixteen cases of the Finnish noun. Odd, some languages get by with only singular and plural. The American Indian languages even failed to distinguish number. Except Sioux, in which there was a plural only for animate objects. The blue room was round and warm and smooth. No way to say warm in French. There was only hot and tepid If there's no word for it, how do you think about it? And, if there isn't the proper form, you don't have the how even if you have the words. Imagine, in Spanish having to assign a sex to every object: dog, table, tree, can-opener. Imagine, in Hungarian, not being able to assign a sex to anything: he, she, it all the same word. Thou art my friend, but you are my king; thus the distinctions of Elizabeth the First's English. But with some oriental languages, which all but dispense with gender and number, you are my friend, you are my parent, and YOU are my priest, and YOU are my king, and YOU are my servant, and YOU are my servant whom I'm going to fire tomorrow if YOU don't watch it, and YOU are my king whose policies I totally disagree with and have sawdust in YOUR head instead of brains, YOUR highness, and YOU may be my friend, but I'm still gonna smack YOU up side the head if YOU ever say that to me again;<br />And who the hell are you anyway . . .?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/378714-abstract-thoughts-in-a-blue-room-nominative-genitive-etative-accusative">26 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Sometimes you want to say things, and you're missing an idea to make them with, and missing a word to make the idea with. In the beginning was the word. That's how somebody tried to explain it once. Until something is named, it doesn't exist.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/653824-sometimes-you-want-to-say-things-and-you-re-missing-an">24 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/13612561-babel-17">More quotes&hellip;</a>
